Validity of Reconciliation After Court Judgment
Rulings on sulh after judgment with varying amounts and timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a settlement (sulh) for more, less, or different property is valid or invalid after a court judgment for diya.

Core Idea
A settlement for more than the judgment is invalid if on credit; valid if immediate and in specific goods.

This passage from al-Dhahiriyyah presents nuanced rulings on reconciliation (sulh) after a court has decreed blood money (diya). It distinguishes between settlements for more, less, or different property, and between credit and immediate exchange. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying the Hanafi position on valid compromises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment and the proposed settlement
Note the amount decreed (e.g., 10,000 dirhams or 100 camels) and the amount agreed in sulh (e.g., 15,000 dirhams or 150 camels). Determine whether the settlement is for more, less, or a different type.
Focus on the increase or decrease relative to the judgment.
2
Classify the settlement by timing and specificity
Determine if the settlement is on credit (nasi'ah) or immediate (yadan bi yad). If immediate, check if the items are specified (e.g., specific camels).
Credit in same genus may involve riba; immediate exchange with specified items is akin to a sale.
3
Apply the rule on increase
If the settlement is for more than the judgment: on credit → invalid (riba); immediate and specified → valid. If less than the judgment: always valid. If different type: valid even if more.
The key is whether the increase is due to riba or a legitimate trade.
4
Consider the timing of sulh: after judgment vs. before
The rules apply after judgment or after the parties are satisfied. If sulh occurs before judgment, a settlement for more than the diya is invalid.
The rationale differs: pre-judgment settlement for more may be seen as a waiver or admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : وإن وقع الصلح على خمسة عشر ألفاً بعد قضاء القاضي بعشرة آلاف فهذا الصلح باطل لما فيه من الزيادة على الدية، وإن كان مائة المقضي به من الإبل فاصطلحا على مائة وخمسين إن وقع الصلح نسيئة لا شك أنه لا يجوز، وإن كان يداً بيد إن كان الإبل بأعيانها ثم اصطلحوا على مائة وخمسين من الإبل بأعيانها كان ذلك جائزاً هذا إذا وقع الصلح على أكثر من النوع الذي وقع به القضاء، أما إذا وقع الصلح على أقل مما وقع به القضاء فإنه يجوز حالاً ونسيئة، وإذا اصطلحا على خلاف جنس ما وقع به القضاء وقد صالحه على أكثر مما قضى به فإنه يجوز. هذا الذي ذكرنا إذا اصطلحا بعد القضاء أو الرضا، أما إذا اصطلحا قبل القضاء إن كان المصالح عليه أكثر من الدية فإنه لا يجوز.
And in al-Dhahiriyyah: If a settlement is made for fifteen thousand after the judge had decreed ten thousand, this settlement is invalid due to the increase over the diya. If the decreed amount was one hundred camels, and they settle for one hundred and fifty – if the settlement is on credit, there is no doubt it is not permissible. If it is hand-to-hand (immediate), and the camels are specific individuals, and they settle for one hundred and fifty of specific camels, that is permissible. This applies when the settlement is for more than the type decreed. But if the settlement is for less than the decreed amount, it is permissible both immediately and on credit. If they settle for a different type than what was decreed, and it is for more than the decreed value, it is permissible. This is when they settle after the judgment or after satisfaction. But if they settle before the judgment, if the settlement amount is more than the diya, it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Dhahiriyyah provides detailed rules on the validity of reconciliation (sulh) after a court has already issued a judgment for diya. The key principle: A settlement for more than the judgment is generally invalid if it is on credit, because it involves riba (usury) since diya is a debt. However, if the settlement is immediate (hand-to-hand) and the items are specific (e.g., specific camels), then it is permissible because it is considered a sale. If the settlement is for less than the judgment, it is always permissible. If the settlement is for a different type (e.g., cash instead of camels) and for more than the value, it is also permissible. These rules apply after judgment or after the parties have agreed voluntarily. If settlement is attempted before judgment, settling for more than the diya is invalid, likely because it would be an admission or a waiver of rights.

Key Terms
نسيئة (nasī’ah): Credit or deferred payment basis.
يداً بيد (yadan bi yad): Hand-to-hand, i.e., immediate exchange.
ديَة (diyah): Blood money, compensation paid to the heirs of a victim.
صُلح (sulh): Settlement, compromise, reconciliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a settlement for more than the court-decreed diya valid?
A) Never.
B) Only if on credit.
C) If it is immediate and in specific items of the same type.
D) If it is for a different type of property.

Key Takeaway
A settlement after judgment for more than the decreed amount is invalid on credit due to riba, but valid if immediate and in specified goods; settlements for less or different types are valid.

Compensation for Intercourse with Another's Slave-Girl under Shubha
Ruling on compensation calculation for shubha intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for compensating the owner of a slave-girl when intercourse occurs under shubha by comparing mahr al-mithl and the reduction in value due to defloration.

Core Idea
Compensation for shubha intercourse with a slave-girl is the higher of mahr al-mithl or the loss of value from defloration.

This passage from al-Khaniyya addresses the financial liability when a man mistakenly has intercourse with another's slave-girl (shubha) or simply deflowers her. The Hanafi school, through Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, provides a precise calculation to ensure the owner is not undercompensated. The ruling prioritizes actual loss and prevents double recovery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is wati' (intercourse) under shubha (mistaken belief that the slave-girl is permissible) or removal of hymen (izalat al-bikara). The victim is a slave-girl owned by another person.
Shubha removes the hadd penalty but does not eliminate financial liability for the damage caused to the owner.
2
Determine the two potential components of compensation
First, mahr al-mithl of the slave-girl — the standard mahr for a woman of similar status. Second, the reduction in the slave-girl's market value due to loss of virginity (nuqsan al-bikara).
Both are quantifiable: mahr al-mithl based on social norms, and the price drop based on market.
3
Compare the two amounts and pay the greater
If the reduction in value is greater than mahr al-mithl, the defendant pays that amount. If mahr al-mithl is greater, he pays that. The lesser amount is considered included in the greater, so no double payment.
This ensures the owner receives full compensation for the actual loss, but no more.
4
Understand the rationale behind the rule
The owner's loss is either the devaluation of his property or the mahr he could have claimed if the slave-girl were married. The higher amount covers the actual damage.
This is a practical application of the principle that compensation must cover actual harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : لو وطئ جارية إنسان بشبهة أو أزال بكارتها فعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ينظر إلى مهر مثلها فيزاد إلى نقصان بكارتها إن كان أكثر يجب ذلك ويدخل الأقل في الأكثر . ولو أن صيباً زنى في صبية وأذهب عذرتها كان عليه المهر بإزالة البكارة لو كانت المرأة بالغة مستكرهة، وإن كانت مطاوعة لا يجب المهر لأنه لو وجب على الصبي كان لولي الصبي أن بذلك عليها كما لو أمر صبياً بشيء يلحقه ضمانه كان لولي الصغير أن يرجع على الأمر فلا يفيد تضمين الصغير
And in al-Khaniyya: If a man had intercourse with a human's slave-girl with shubha or removed her hymen, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, one looks at her mahr al-mithl, and he adds to it the deficiency of her hymen; if it is more, that is obligatory, and the lesser enters into the greater. And if a boy committed zina with a girl and removed her hymen, then upon him is the mahr for removing the hymen if the woman was an adult and forced; but if she was consenting, the mahr is not obligatory, because if it were obligatory upon the boy, the guardian of the boy would have recourse against her for that, just as if he ordered a boy to do something that entails a liability, the guardian of the minor has recourse against the orderer; so the imposition of liability on the minor is of no benefit.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyya addresses two scenarios. First: a man has intercourse with another's slave-girl under a mistaken belief (shubha) or simply deflowers her. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the compensation is calculated by comparing the mahr al-mithl (market value of the slave-girl's mahr) to the reduction in her value due to loss of virginity. He must pay the higher of the two amounts, and if one is less, it is included in the greater. This ensures the owner does not suffer loss. Second: a minor boy (sabi) has zina with a girl and removes her hymen. If the girl was an adult and forced (mustakrahah), the boy must pay mahr for removing the hymen. However, if she was consenting (muta'wi'ah), no mahr is due. The reason: if the boy were liable, his guardian could sue the girl for ordering or causing the act, making the liability circular. This ruling protects minors from financial obligations arising from acts they are not fully accountable for.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): A mistaken belief that intercourse was lawful, e.g., believing the woman was his own wife.
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard mahr for a woman of similar status in that society.
نقصان البكارة (nuqsan al-bikara): The reduction in the slave-girl's market value due to loss of virginity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on shubha intercourse with a slave-girl, how is compensation determined?
A) Pay the full mahr al-mithl only.
B) Pay the greater of mahr al-mithl or the reduction in value from defloration.
C) Pay both mahr al-mithl and the reduction in value.
D) Pay the reduction in value only.

Key Takeaway
Compensation for shubha intercourse with a slave-girl is the higher of mahr al-mithl or the loss in value from defloration, ensuring full but not double recovery.

Liability of a Minor Boy for Defloration: Consent and Recourse
Ruling on minor boy's liability for defloration depending on woman's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a minor boy must pay mahr for defloration and explain how the woman's consent and the concept of recourse affect liability.

Core Idea
A minor boy pays mahr for defloration only if the woman was forced; if consenting, no mahr due because his guardian would sue her.

The second part of the passage from al-Khaniyya addresses a unique scenario: a minor boy (sabi) commits zina with a girl and removes her hymen. The ruling distinguishes based on the woman's consent and uses the principle of recourse (ruju') to protect minors from liability when the other party bears responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
The actors are a minor boy (sabi) and a woman. The act is zina with defloration (removal of hymen). The woman's status (adult or not, forced or consenting) is critical.
The text specifies that the woman is an adult (baligha).
2
Check the woman's consent
If the woman was forced (mustakrahah), the boy is liable for mahr for removing the hymen. If she was consenting (muta'awi'ah), the boy is not liable.
Forced means she did not consent; consenting means she willingly participated.
3
Understand the rationale for the distinction
If the boy were liable even when the woman consented, his guardian (wali) could claim recourse against her for causing the act. This would create a circular situation: the boy pays, then the guardian sues her, making the boy's liability ineffective. Therefore, the law does not impose liability on the minor in the consenting case.
The analogy: if someone orders a minor to do something that incurs liability, the guardian can sue the orderer.
4
Apply the rule to analogous cases
This principle protects minors from financial obligations when the counterparty's own actions contributed to the harm. It balances protection of minors with fairness to victims.
The rule is specific to minors; adults would be liable regardless of consent in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : لو وطئ جارية إنسان بشبهة أو أزال بكارتها فعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ينظر إلى مهر مثلها فيزاد إلى نقصان بكارتها إن كان أكثر يجب ذلك ويدخل الأقل في الأكثر . ولو أن صيباً زنى في صبية وأذهب عذرتها كان عليه المهر بإزالة البكارة لو كانت المرأة بالغة مستكرهة، وإن كانت مطاوعة لا يجب المهر لأنه لو وجب على الصبي كان لولي الصبي أن بذلك عليها كما لو أمر صبياً بشيء يلحقه ضمانه كان لولي الصغير أن يرجع على الأمر فلا يفيد تضمين الصغير
And in al-Khaniyya: If a man had intercourse with a human's slave-girl with shubha or removed her hymen, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, one looks at her mahr al-mithl, and he adds to it the deficiency of her hymen; if it is more, that is obligatory, and the lesser enters into the greater. And if a boy committed zina with a girl and removed her hymen, then upon him is the mahr for removing the hymen if the woman was an adult and forced; but if she was consenting, the mahr is not obligatory, because if it were obligatory upon the boy, the guardian of the boy would have recourse against her for that, just as if he ordered a boy to do something that entails a liability, the guardian of the minor has recourse against the orderer; so the imposition of liability on the minor is of no benefit.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyya addresses two scenarios. First: a man has intercourse with another's slave-girl under a mistaken belief (shubha) or simply deflowers her. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the compensation is calculated by comparing the mahr al-mithl (market value of the slave-girl's mahr) to the reduction in her value due to loss of virginity. He must pay the higher of the two amounts, and if one is less, it is included in the greater. This ensures the owner does not suffer loss. Second: a minor boy (sabi) has zina with a girl and removes her hymen. If the girl was an adult and forced (mustakrahah), the boy must pay mahr for removing the hymen. However, if she was consenting (muta'wi'ah), no mahr is due. The reason: if the boy were liable, his guardian could sue the girl for ordering or causing the act, making the liability circular. This ruling protects minors from financial obligations arising from acts they are not fully accountable for.

Key Terms
صبي (sabi): A minor boy who has not reached puberty.
مستكرهة (mustakrahah): A woman who is forced or coerced into the act.
مطاوعة (muta'awi'ah): A woman who consents willingly.
ولي الصبي (wali al-sabi): The guardian of the minor boy, typically his father or legal custodian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling, when does a minor boy have to pay mahr for defloration?
A) Always, if he caused the defloration.
B) If the woman is adult and consenting.
C) If the woman is adult and forced.
D) Never, because minors are not liable.

Key Takeaway
A minor boy's liability for defloration mahr is contingent on the woman being forced; consent removes liability due to the guardian's right of recourse against her.

Placing Structures in Public and Private Roads: Rulings and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preventing structures in public roads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on placing structures in public versus private roads and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on who may prevent such placement.

Core Idea
In a public road, use is mubah unless it harms; but who may prevent? Abu Hanifa: any individual if no Imam's permission; Muhammad: no one unless harm.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding what a person may place in roads. It presents a general ruling that placement is permissible as long as it does not cause harm, and then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about who has the right to prevent such placement. The discussion distinguishes between public thoroughfares (nafidh) and private cul-de-sacs (ghayr nafidh), setting out the rights of individuals and the role of the Imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two road types
Distinguish between a public thoroughfare (al-tariq al-nafidh) and a non-public road (ghayr al-nafidh). Note the ownership: public road is owned by no one (milk al-ammah); private road is jointly owned by its residents.
This distinction is crucial for the permissibility and permission requirements.
2
State the general ruling on placement
In a public road, placing a structure (like a bench or projection) is mubah (permissible) if it does not cause harm. The hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' is the basis. In a private road, placement requires unanimous consent of all residents.
The text uses an analogy with debt repayment and sitting for trade to illustrate the harm condition.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on prevention
The core dispute: who may prevent someone from placing a structure in a public road? The three positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say any individual may prevent if done without Imam's permission; Muhammad says no one may prevent if there is no harm.
The key difference is the weight given to the Imam's authority versus the general permissibility from Shari'ah.
4
Understand the Hanafi response to Muhammad
The text argues that using a road for a purpose other than passage (like placing a structure) is analogous to trespass; even if permissible in itself, people have the right to object, unlike passage which is the road's intended use.
This response establishes that the permissibility does not negate the right to prevent.
5
Compare public vs private road requirements
For public roads, unanimous consent is impractical, so each individual is treated as a representative of the public. For private roads, because consent is possible, it must be obtained from all co-owners.
This difference explains why the rules differ between the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب ما يحدث الرجل في الطريق

منه متعنت فلا
فيه إلا بإذنهم فإن مات أحد بسقوطها فديته على عاقلته كما لو حفر بئراً في طريق أو وضع حجراً فتلف به إنسان ولو بهيمة فضمانها في ماله ومن جعل بالوعة في طريق لا ضرر ولا ضرار في الإسلام (۱) وهذا نظير من عليه الدين فإنه لا يسعه التأخير إذا طالبه، فلو لم يطالبه جاز له تأخيره وعلى هذا القعود في الطريق للبيع والشراء يجوز إن لم يضر بأحد، وأن أضر لم يجز لما قلنا وأما الخصومة فيه فقال أبو حنيفة: لكل واحد من عرض الناس أن يمنعه من الوضع وأن يكلفه الرفع بعد الوضع سواء كان فيه ضرر ولم يكن إذا وضع بغير إذن الإمام لافتياته على رأيه لأن التدبير في أمور العامة إلى الإمام. والعُرض بالضم الناحية والمراد واحد من الناس. وعلى قول أبي يوسف لكل واحد أن يمنعه من ذلك، وعلى قول محمد ليس لأحد أن يمنعه قبل الوضع ولا بعده إذا لم يكن فيه ضرر الناس لأنه مأذون له في إحداثه شرعاً ألا ترى أنه يجوز له ذلك إن لم يمنعه والمانع يمكن من ذلك فصار كما لو أذن له الإمام بل أولى لأن إذن الشارع أحرى ولاية وأقوى يجوز لأحد أن يمنعه. وجوابه أن هذا انتفاع بما لم توضع له الطريق فكان كالمرور حتى لهم منعه وإن كان جائزاً في نفسه بخلاف المرور فيه لأنه انتفاع بما وضع له فلان يكون الله : (وله التصرف في النافذ إلا إذا أضر) أي له أن ف بإحداث الجرصن وغيره مما تقدم ذكره في الطريق النافذ إذا لم يضر بالعامة معناه إذا لم يمنعه أحد وقد ذكرناه. والخلاف الذي فيه فلا نعيده قال رحمه الله: (وفي غيره لا يتصرف فيه إلا إنهم) أي في غير النافذ من الطريق لا يتصرف أحد بإحداث ما ذكرنا إلا بإذن أهله لأن الطريق التي ليست بنافذة مملوكة لأهلها فهم فيها شركاء ولهذا يستحقون بها الشفعة والتصرف في الملك المشترك من الوجه الذي لم يوضع له لا يملك إلا بإذن الكل أضر أو لم يضر بهم بخلاف النافذ لأنه ليس لأحد فيه ملك فيجوز الانتفاع به ما لم يضر بأحد، ولأنه إذا كان حق العامة فيتعذر الوصول إلى إذن الكل فجعل كل واحد كأنه المالك وحده في حق الانتفاع. هو

.(۳۲۷
Book of Diyat: Chapter on what a person causes in the road. If someone is aggressive, there is no... except with their permission. If someone dies from its fall, his blood-money is on his 'aqila, just as if he dug a well in the road or placed a stone and a person perished from it, and if an animal, its compensation is from his wealth. Whoever makes a cesspit in the road: 'No harm nor reciprocating harm in Islam.' This is similar to one who owes a debt: he may not delay it if the creditor demands, but if not demanded he may delay. Likewise, sitting in the road for selling and buying is permissible if it does not harm anyone, and if it harms, it is not permissible. As for dispute about this: Abu Hanifa said: Every single person of the side of the people may prevent him from placing and may compel him to remove after placing, whether there is harm or not, if he placed without the Imam's permission due to infringing upon his opinion, because management of public affairs is to the Imam. 'Al-'urd' (with damm) means the side, and the intended meaning is one of the people. According to Abu Yusuf's saying: every single person may prevent him from that. According to Muhammad's saying: no one may prevent him before placing nor after, if there is no harm to the people, because he is permitted by Shari'ah to create it. Do you not see that it is permissible for him if no one prevents, and the preventer can do that? So it becomes as if the Imam permitted him, rather more so because the permission of the Lawgiver is more deserving of authority and stronger. Response: This is using the road for a purpose other than what it was made for, so it is like passing through, so they have the right to prevent him, even though it is permissible in itself, unlike passing through because it is using for its purpose. So God says: 'And he has the right to dispose in the thoroughfare unless he harms' i.e. he may create a projection and other things previously mentioned in the public thoroughfare if he does not harm the public, meaning if no one prevents him, and we have mentioned it. The disagreement about it we will not repeat. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And in other than it he may not dispose without their permission' i.e. in non-public roads, no one may dispose by creating what we mentioned except with the permission of its residents, because the road that is not thoroughfare is owned by its residents, so they are partners in it, and therefore they deserve pre-emption by it, and disposal in joint property in a manner not intended for it is not permissible except with permission of all, whether harm or not, unlike the public road because no one has ownership in it, so using it is permissible as long as no one is harmed, and because it is a right of the public and it is impossible to obtain permission from all, so each one is made as if he is the sole owner in terms of benefit. It is not so in non-public road because reaching their consent is possible, so it remains on its partnership in reality and legally. In al-Muntaqa: He is only ordered to remove these things if their occurrence is known, so if they are old, no one has the right to remove, and if the state of these things is unknown, they are considered old, and this is the principle. Narrated by Ibn Majah in the Book of Judgments, Chapter 17, al-Muwatta in the Book of Judgments, Hadith 31. Ahmad in his Musnad (5/327).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding what a person may cause in the road. The text begins by stating the general ruling: any structure placed in a public road (al-tariq al-nafidh) that causes harm is prohibited, based on the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar fi al-Islam' (No harm nor reciprocating harm in Islam). It draws an analogy with debt repayment – delay is permissible only if the creditor does not demand. Similarly, sitting in the road for trade is allowed if no harm results. Then it presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding who may prevent a person from placing such a structure: Abu Hanifa holds that any individual (from the 'urd, i.e., the side of the people) may prevent and compel removal, even without harm, if done without the Imam's permission, because public management belongs to the Imam. Abu Yusuf agrees that anyone may prevent. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that no one may prevent before or after placement if there is no harm, because Shari'ah permits it; the permission of the Lawgiver is stronger than that of the Imam. The Hanafi response (likely that of the author) is that using the road for a purpose other than its intended use (like placing a projection) is like mere passage, so people have the right to prevent even if the act is permissible in itself, unlike passage which is for the road's purpose. The text then cites the matn: 'He may dispose in the thoroughfare unless he harms,' meaning unless someone prevents him. For non-public roads (ghayr nafidh), no one may act except with permission of all residents because it is jointly owned; this contrasts with public roads where no ownership exists and unanimous consent is impractical, so each individual is treated as owner for permission. The passage ends by quoting al-Muntaqa that removal is only for new structures; old ones are left undisturbed, as a principle.

Key Terms
الطريق النافذ (al-tariq al-nafidh): A public thoroughfare that leads to a through road, not a dead end; owned by no one, right of passage for all.
غير النافذ (ghayr al-nafidh): A private road or cul-de-sac owned jointly by the residents; everyone needs permission from all co-owners to place structures.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (la darar wa la dirar): A hadith principle: no person may cause harm to another, and no reciprocation of harm; used as proof for prohibiting harmful acts in public roads.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when may an individual prevent someone from placing a structure in a public road?
A) Only if the structure causes harm to passersby.
B) Only if the Imam has not given permission.
C) If the structure was placed without the Imam's permission, even without harm.
D) Never; only the Imam can prevent.

Key Takeaway
In a public road, placement is mubah if harmless, but per Abu Hanifa, any individual may prevent it if done without the Imam's permission; in a private road, unanimous consent is required.

Liability After Sale: Projections vs. Leaning Walls
Ruling on liability after sale for road obstruction vs. leaning wall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a road obstruction after sale and distinguish it from the leaning wall case by identifying the effective cause ('illa).

Core Idea
Liability for obstructing the road is based on the act, not ownership; selling the property does not extinguish liability.

Imagine a person builds a balcony that projects into a public street and later sells the entire building. The balcony collapses and kills a pedestrian. Who is liable? In Hanafi fiqh, the original seller remains liable, not the buyer. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and contrasts it with the case of a leaning wall, where liability shifts differently. By understanding the 'illa (effective cause), you will grasp a key principle of Hanafi tort law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two distinct cases: (1) a projection (wing/beam) into the road that causes death after sale, and (2) a leaning wall that falls after sale and warning (ishhad). State the ruling for each.
The first case leads to seller liability; the second leads to no liability for either party.
2
Locate the 'illa for each case
For the projection, the effective cause ('illa) is the act of obstructing the public road (ishghal al-tariq). For the leaning wall, the 'illa is the failure to give warning (ishhad) after ownership transfer. Note how the 'illa differs.
In the projection case, the obstruction persists after sale; in the leaning wall case, the warning lapses because ownership is required for valid ishhad.
3
Apply the analogy to non-owners
The author argues that if a non-owner (lessee, borrower, usurper) had created the obstruction, they would be liable for the same act. This shows that liability for road obstruction is independent of ownership.
This qiyas (analogy) reinforces that the seller remains liable after sale.
4
Contrast with the leaning wall exception
In the leaning wall case, a non-owner is not liable because liability hinges on valid warning (ishhad), which only an owner can give. Thus, the ruling differs.
The key exception: for leaning walls, ownership is central to the liability mechanism.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Summarize: In road obstruction cases, the person who performed the act of obstruction (original tortfeasor) remains liable even after selling the property. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.
The proof is strong because it is based on the continued existence of the harmful act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أشرع جناحاً إلى الطريق ثم باع الكل فأصاب الجناح رجلاً فقتله أو وضع خشبة في الطريق ثم باع الخشبة وتركها المشتري حتى عطب بها إنسان فالضمان على البائع لأن فعله لم ينفسخ بزوال ملكه وهو الموجب بخلاف الحائط المائل إذا باعه بعد الإشهاد عليه ثم سقط في ملك المشتري على إنسان حيث لا يضمن البائع ولا المشتري لأن المشتري لم يشهد عليه وهو شرط الحائط المائل، وفي حق البائع قد بطل الإشهاد الأول لأن الملك شرط لصحة الإشهاد فيبطل بخروجه عن ملكه لأنه لا يتمكن من نقض ملك الغير، وفيما نحن فيه إنما يضمن بإشغال الطريق لا باعتبار الملك والإشغال باق بعد البيع ألا ترى أن ذلك الإشغال لو حصل من غير مالك كالمستأجر أو المعير أو الغاصب يضمن وفي الحائط لا يضمن غير المالك
And if he projects a wing (structure) into the road then sells the entire property, and the wing strikes a person and kills him, or he places a wooden beam in the road then sells the beam and the buyer leaves it until a person is harmed by it, the liability is on the seller, because his act is not annulled by the removal of his ownership, and it is the cause. Unlike a leaning wall if he sells it after having given warning (ishhad) then it falls in the property of the buyer on a person, where neither the seller nor the buyer is liable, because the buyer did not give warning, which is a condition for the leaning wall, and for the seller, the first warning is void because ownership is a condition for the validity of warning, so it becomes void when it leaves his ownership, as he is not able to demolish the property of another. In our case here, he is liable due to obstructing the road, not due to ownership, and the obstruction remains after the sale. Do you not see that if that obstruction came from a non-owner like a lessee, borrower, or usurper, he would be liable? In the wall case, a non-owner is not liable.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person projects a structure (like a balcony or beam) into the public road, then sells the entire property. After the sale, the projection kills someone. The ruling is that the original seller (who placed the projection) remains liable, not the buyer. The reason is that liability arises from the act of obstructing the road (ishghal al-tariq), not from ownership. The act of obstruction persists even after ownership transfers. The author contrasts this with a leaning wall (ha'it ma'il) that falls after sale: if the seller had given legal warning (ishhad) before selling, but then sells, the warning becomes void because ownership is necessary for the validity of ishhad, and the seller can no longer demolish the wall on the buyer's property. Therefore, in the leaning wall case, neither seller nor buyer is liable. In contrast, for a projection in the road, the basis of liability is the continued obstruction of the public way, which is independent of ownership. Even a non-owner (lessee, borrower, usurper) would be liable for such obstruction.

Key Terms
إشغال الطريق (ishghal al-tariq): Occupying or obstructing the public road with a structure or object, causing a hazard.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): Legal warning or testimony given formally, e.g., for a leaning wall to alert authorities and transfer liability if it falls.
الحائط المائل (al-ha'it al-ma'il): A wall that is leaning and poses a risk of collapse; special rules apply for liability after warning and sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the seller remain liable after selling a property that has a projection into the road?
A) Because the seller still owns the projection.
B) Because the liability is based on the act of obstruction, which continues after sale.
C) Because the buyer is not aware of the projection.
D) Because the seller must have given ishhad.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for road obstruction remains with the one who created it, even after ownership is transferred.

Liability for Building in the Public Road: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Explaining the ramification of orderer's liability before and after completion of work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the orderer's liability for work in the public road and explain how istihsan overrides qiyas based on the orderer's benefit after completion.

Core Idea
When a worker builds illegally in a public road, the orderer is liable after completion due to istihsan, not qiyas.

This passage addresses a classic fiqh problem: a person hires workers to build a house in the middle of a public road, an illegal act. The workers know it's illegal. What happens if the house falls and damages property? Should the workers or the orderer pay? The text presents two approaches: strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan). Understanding the interplay between these two methods is essential for grasping Hanafi legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in qiyas
According to qiyas, since the workers knew the order was invalid (the orderer cannot directly build in the road), they are liable for any damage and cannot claim recourse from the orderer, even after completion.
This is based on the analogy to slaughtering a sheep for another without permission: the slaughterer is liable.
2
Identify the ruling in istihsan
In istihsan, after the work is completed, liability shifts to the orderer because the orderer benefits from the structure (even though it was illegally built).
Istihsan overrides qiyas because of the orderer's actual benefit after completion.
3
Understand the rationale for istihsan
The orderer's order is invalid only because he cannot benefit from the public road initially. After completion, he does benefit, creating a semblance of validity that makes it more appropriate to hold him liable.
Before completion, the orderer has no benefit, so the worker is liable; after completion, the orderer's benefit triggers liability.
4
Compare with the slaughter analogy
In the slaughter analogy, the hirer never benefits from the slaughtered animal (the sheep belongs to the neighbor), so liability stays with the slaughterer. In the building case, the orderer does benefit after completion, distinguishing it.
This shows why istihsan applies only after completion, not before.
5
Apply to similar cases
The same principle applies to other causes of damage in the road, like a falling waterspout or a dug well: the one who caused it is liable, but expiation is not required because it is killing by cause.
This reinforces that liability is based on causation and benefit, not on the legality of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرجعوا به على الآمر قياساً، وإن سقط بعد الفراغ من العمل فكذلك في جواب القياس لأن المستأجر أمرهم بما لا يملك مباشرته بنفسه وقد علموا فساد أمره فلم يحكم بالضمان على المستأجر كما لو استأجر رجلاً ليذبح شاة جاز له وأعلمه فذبح ثم ضمن الذابح للجار لم يرجع به على الآمر ، وكذا لو استأجرهم ليبنوا له بيتاً في وسط الطريق ثم سقط وأتلف شيئاً يرجعوا به على الآمر وفي الاستحسان يكون الضمان على الأمر لأن هذا الأمر صحيح من حيث إنه لا يجوز بيعه فمن حيث إن الأمر صحيح يكون إقرار الضمان على الأمر بعد الفراغ من العمل، ومن حيث إنه فاسد يكون الضمان على العامل قبل الفراغ من العلم عملاً بها، وإظهار شبهة الصحة بعد الفراغ من العلم أولى من إظهاره قبل الفراغ لأن أمر الأمر إنما لا يصلح من حيث إنه لا يملك الانتفاع بفناء داره وإنما حصل له ذلك بعد الفراغ من العمل. قوله «كما لو حفر بئراً في طريق فتلف به إنسان أي القتل بسقوط الميزاب ونحوه كالقتل بحفر البئر ووضع الحجر في الطريق لأن كل واحد منهما قتل بسبب حتى لا تجب فيه الكفارة ولا يحرم الميزاب فيكون حكمه كحكمه فيما ذكرناه
They would claim recourse against the orderer by analogy, and if it fell after the completion of the work, similarly in the answer of analogy, because the hirer ordered them to do what he himself could not directly do, and they knew the invalidity of his order, so no liability was imposed on the hirer, just as if he hired a man to slaughter a sheep, which is allowed for him, and told him, and he slaughtered, then the slaughterer compensated the neighbor, he does not have recourse against the orderer. Likewise, if he hired them to build him a house in the middle of the road, then it fell and destroyed something, they would have recourse against the orderer. And in istihsan, liability is on the orderer, because this order is valid insofar as it is not permissible to sell it, so from the aspect that the order is valid, it establishes liability on the orderer after completion of the work, and from the aspect that it is invalid, liability is on the worker before completion, acting on it. And manifesting the semblance of validity after completion is more appropriate than manifesting it before completion, because the orderer's order is invalid only because he does not have the right to benefit from the courtyard of his house, and that is only achieved after completion of the work. His saying: 'Just as if he dug a well in the road and a person perished by it' meaning killing by a falling waterspout and the like, like killing by digging a well and placing a stone in the road, because each of them is killing by a cause, so that expiation is not obligatory, and the waterspout is not prohibited, so its ruling is like its ruling in what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the liability when someone hires workers to build something in a public road. The passage first presents the ruling by qiyas (analogy): if the workers build the house and it falls before completion, they are liable; if after completion, they still have recourse against the orderer? The text is a bit confusing. Actually the qiyas says that if the workers are ordered to do something illegal (building in the road), and they know it's illegal, they are liable, and they cannot get recourse from the orderer. But istihsan says that after completion, the orderer benefits from the structure (even though it's illegal), so liability shifts to the orderer. The passage then draws an analogy to a waterspout falling, or a stone placed in the road, as similar causes of death that do not require expiation. The author follows the order of the matn, expanding on the ruling with both qiyas and istihsan, concluding that the istihsan view is preferred after completion.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in cases where strict analogy would lead to hardship or go against public interest.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to pay compensation for damage or destruction of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling in istihsan when a hired worker builds a house in the middle of a public road, the house falls after completion, and destroys property?
A) The worker is liable and has no recourse against the orderer.
B) The orderer is liable.
C) Both are not liable because the act was illegal.
D) The orderer is liable only if the worker was unaware.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a worker builds illegally in a public road, the orderer is liable after completion because of istihsan, overriding the qiyas rule that would hold the worker liable.

Two Diggers: Liability When Deepening a Well
Ruling on liability when two people dig the same well at different levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between qiyas and istihsan regarding liability when a second person deepens a well dug by another, and apply the ruling when the well mouth is widened.

Core Idea
Liability for a deadly well depends on whose digging directly caused the fall.

This lesson explores a nuanced case from Hanafi tort law: two people dig the same well at different levels. What happens when a victim falls in? The Hanafi school has two views, based on qiyas and istihsan, and the answer also depends on whether the second digger widened the mouth. Understanding these rulings sharpens your ability to trace causation in mutual liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Read the passage and identify the two main scenarios: (1) first digs the well, second deepens it; (2) first digs the well, second widens its mouth. Note the common elements: a public road, a fall resulting in death.
2
State Muhammad's position (qiyas)
According to qiyas, the first digger alone is liable. This is the view taken by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. Identify the reasoning: the first created the danger; the second only added to it.
Qiyas analogy: the original case of a single digger being liable is extended to this case.
3
State the istihsan position
By istihsan, both diggers are liable in thirds. Understand that istihsan overrides the strict analogy for a more equitable result, recognizing the second's contribution as a direct cause.
The division 'aththāthan' implies shared liability proportionally.
4
Examine the variation (widening the mouth)
When the second widens the mouth, liability depends on where the victim fell: if in the widened part alone, the second is liable; if partly from both, both are liable; if unknown, both are liable.
This shows causation is key: the liable party is the one whose digging directly caused the fatal fall.
5
Analyze the underlying principle
Both opinions agree that direct causation determines liability. The dispute is whether the second's deepening is a sufficiently direct cause to share liability. The widening case clarifies that when the fall is traceable to the second's work, the second bears full or partial liability.
Istihsan aims at fairness, not strict analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «حفر إلى آخره» حفر بئراً في الطريق فجاء آخر وحفر طائفة في أسفلها ثم وقع فيها إنسان ومات في القياس يضمن الأول وبه أخذ محمد وفي الاستحسان يجب الضمان عليهما أثلاثاً. ولو حفر بئراً ثم جاء رأسها فسقط فيها إنسان ومات كان الضمان عليهما أثلاثاً قالوا : تأويل المسألة أن الثاني وسع ) رأسها بحيث يعلم الناس أن الواقع إنما وقع في موضع بعضه من حفر الأول وبعضه من حفر الثاني، أما إذا وسع الثاني رأسها بحيث إنه إنما وقع في موضع حفر الثاني كان الضمان على الثاني وإن لم يدر فالضمان عليهما.
His saying 'dig to its end': He dug a well in the road, then another came and dug a portion at its bottom, then a person fell into it and died. By qiyas, the first is liable, and Muhammad took that view. By istihsan, liability is imposed on both in thirds. And if he dug a well, then another widened its mouth, and a person fell into it and died, the liability is on both in thirds. They said: The interpretation of the issue is that the second widened its mouth such that people know that the falling only occurred in a place partly from the first's dig and partly from the second's dig. But if the second widened its mouth such that it fell only in the place of the second's dig, then the liability is on the second, and if it is not known, then liability is on both.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about liability when two people dig the same well: one digs the original well, another deepens it. According to qiyas (and the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani), only the first digger is liable because the second's digging was an addition to the original well that already existed. However, istihsan holds both liable in thirds: the first for the well itself, the second for deepening it. The passage then discusses a variation: if the second widens the mouth of the well, and the fall occurs in the widened part, liability is on the second alone; if it's unclear, both are liable. The ruling follows the principle that liability is based on the direct cause of the fall.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas for a more equitable result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two diggers (first digs well, second deepens it), what is the view of Muhammad al-Shaybani?
A) Both are liable in thirds.
B) The first digger is liable.
C) The second digger is liable.
D) Neither is liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when two diggers contribute to a well, liability is determined by direct causation: if the victim falls in the part dug by the second, the second is solely liable; otherwise both may share liability, and the school has multiple valid opinions.

Liability When Victim Jumps into Well & Covering Well
Ruling on liability when victim jumps in or well is covered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a victim intentionally jumps into a well and explain the reasoning based on al-zahir.

Core Idea
A digger is not liable if the victim intentionally jumps; the apparent action of a sighted person avoids wells.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two liability scenarios involving wells dug in the road. The first addresses the claim of intentional jumping; the second addresses covering the well. The key legal tool is the concept of al-zahir (the apparent) and the principle that liability is not imposed by doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Rulings
The passage has two parts: (1) victim falls and digger claims jumping; (2) well is covered and another removes the cover. Identify each scenario and the ruling on liability.
2
Analyze the Reasoning for the First Ruling
Understand why the digger's claim is accepted: the apparent (al-zahir) is that a sighted person sees the well and avoids it, so accidental fall is unlikely. Doubt prevents imposing liability.
The text also notes that it is apparent that a person does not throw himself unless in distress, but doubt still prevails.
3
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf had an earlier view, but later agreed with Muhammad. The later view is the relied-upon position: the digger's word is accepted.
This shows the evolution of a jurist's opinion.
4
Apply the Reasoning to the Second Ruling
When the digger covers the well with earth or grass, or someone else removes a cover, the original digger is liable. The restriction 'so if he perished in it' means death must be from the fall.
The digger's active creation of danger (digging) creates ongoing liability unless the victim's intentional act breaks causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاضيخان قوله «حفر إلى آخره» سقط إنسان فقال الحافر إنه ألقى نفسه وكذبه الورثة في ذلك كان القول قول الحافر في قول أبي يوسف أخراً وهو قول محمد لأن الظاهر أن البصير يرى موضع قدميه، وإن كان الظاهر أن الإنسان لا يوقع نفسه إلا إذا وقعت له شدة فلا يجب الضمان بالشك. قوله حفر بئراً في الطريق ثم كساها بالتراب أو بخضر أو بما هو من جنس الأرض يضمن الأول، ولو غطى رأسها وجاء آخر ورفع الغطاء فوقع فيها إنسان ضمن الأول، وقال قاضيخان : قيد بقوله «فتلف فيه
Qadikhan said: His saying 'dig to its end': A person fell, and the digger said he threw himself, and the heirs denied that. The digger's word is accepted in the later opinion of Abu Yusuf, and it is the opinion of Muhammad, because the apparent is that a sighted person sees his footsteps. Even though the apparent is that a person does not throw himself unless he is in distress, so liability is not imposed by doubt. His saying: He dug a well in the road, then covered it with earth or with green or with what is from the same type of soil; the first is liable. And if he covered its mouth and another came and removed the cover, and a person fell into it, the first is liable. And Qadikhan said: He restricted it by his saying 'so if he perished in it'.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First: if the digger claims the victim intentionally threw himself into the well, and the heirs deny this, the digger's statement is taken as true according to the later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This is because a sighted person can see the well and avoid it, so it is not natural to fall accidentally unless one is blind or forced. However, the text notes that it is also apparent that people do not usually throw themselves into wells unless in extreme distress. But since there is doubt, the principle is that liability is not imposed by doubt. Second: if the digger covers the well with earth or grass that matches the ground, he is liable. Also if someone else removes a cover, the original digger remains liable, because the danger is his creation. The restriction 'so if he perished in it' indicates that the death must be from the fall, not from other causes.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zahir): The apparent or presumptive state of affairs; used in legal reasoning to infer what is most likely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, when a person falls into a well and the digger claims the person threw himself in, what is the ruling?
A) The digger must pay diyah.
B) The digger's claim is accepted and he is not liable.
C) Both the digger and the victim's heirs share liability.
D) The matter is left to the judge's discretion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the apparent behavior (al-zahir) can shift the burden of proof; a digger is not liable for a death if the victim likely acted intentionally, due to the principle that liability is not imposed by doubt.

Liability for Wells Based on Location: Private, Public, and Shared Land
Rulings on liability based on digging location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a well dug in one's own property versus a public road, and identify which cases trigger compensation.

Core Idea
A digger is liable only when the well is on public or shared land; digging in private property never incurs liability.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq consolidates the Hanafi position on liability for well-digging. The key determinant is the location of the well. The text also defines the fina' (courtyard) and distinguishes between private, shared, and public spaces. Understanding these categories is essential for applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three land categories
The passage discusses wells dug in: 1) one's own property (ملك), 2) a public road (قارعة الطريق), and 3) shared property like a cul-de-sac (سكة غير نافذة).
Own property includes one's courtyard (فناء) as defined.
2
State the liability for each
Own property: no liability. Public road: full liability. Shared property (cul-de-sac): liability.
3
Understand the definition of fina'
The fina' is the courtyard needed for the house, which may extend into the alley if beneficial. Digging there is considered in one's property if it belongs to the owner.
If the fina' is shared (e.g., neighbor's wall), it may be considered shared property.
4
Note the exception of self-infliction
If the victim throws himself into the well, the digger is not liable, even on public land.
The text states: 'اذا أوقع إنسان نفسه في البئر فلا ضمان على الحافر'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الحفر في فناء داره فوقع فيه إنسان فمات هل يضمن إن كان الفناء لغيره يكون ضامناً، وأما إذا حفر في ملكه أو كان له حق الحفر في القديم فكذا الجواب لا يضمن، وإن لم يكن ملكاً له ولكن كان الجماعة المسلمين أو كان شركاً بأن كان في سكة غير نافذة فإنه يضمن. قال في المنتقى : فناء دار الرجل ما كان في داره يحتاج إليه وإن كان في عرض سكته أو أعرض منها، فأما إذا أمر رجلاً أن يحفر له بئراً في أصل حائط جاره وفنائه فهذا كله فناء الآمر وفناء جاره الذي . هو فناء له فهو فناؤهما، وإن كانت السكة غير نافذة فأمر بالحفر في موضع ليس له فيه منفعة ولا تحتاج إليه الدار وهذا ليس بفنائه، وإذا أوقع إنسان نفسه في البئر فلا ضمان على الحافر. شرح الطحاوي: ومن حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فوقع فيها دابة أو إنسان فتلف فالضمان على الحافر، ولو جاء إنسان فدفعه وألقاه في البئر وهلك فالضمان على الدافع دون الحافر. وفي الخانية : رجل حفر بئراً في ملكه ثم سقط إنسان فقتل الساقط ذلك الإنسان أو الدابة كان الساقط ضامناً دية أو قيمة من كان فيها، وإن كان البئر في الطريق كان الضمان على حافر البئر، فإذا حفر في ملك نفسه فسقوطه لا يكون ضامناً إلى الحافر وكان تلف السقوط عليه مضافاً إلى الساقط . وإذا حفر الرجل بئراً في طريق المسلمين ثم آخر حفر طائقة أخرى في أسفلها ثم وقع إنسان ومات فإنه ينبغي في القياس أن يضمن وبه أخذ محمد، واختلف المشايخ في جواب الاستحسان فمنهم من قال جواب الاستحسان أن يكون الضمان على الأول والثاني، ومنهم من قال جواب الاستحسان أن يكون الضمان على الثاني خاصة إلا أن أصحابنا أخذوا بالقياس وكان كمن حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء إنسان ووضع في البئر سلاحاً ثم جاء إنسان ووقع على السلاح ومات من ذلك فإن الضمان على الحافر وسئل بعضهم عمن حفر في صحراء قرية التي . لأهل القرية وهي مبيت دوابهم حفيرة يضع فيها الحنطة والشعير بغير إذن الباقين فجاء رجل وأوقد في الحفيرة ناراً كستها وذلك أيضاً بغير إذن الباقين فوقع فيها حمار فاحترق بالنار فالضمان على من يجب ؟ فقال : على الحافر. قال : وهذا قياس ما نقل عن أصحابنا في كتاب الديات حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق وألقى رجل فيها حجراً بعدما وقع في البئر رجل فأصابه الحجر الذي في البئر فمات أن الدية على الحافر، ومثله لو وضع رجل حجراً على الأرض بقرب البئر فتعقل فيها إنسان ووقع فهلك فالدية على من وضع الحجر كأنه ألقاه في البئر فمات، ولو كان كذلك كان الضمان على الدافع وكذلك ههنا هذا إذا وضع الحجر واضع، فأما إذا لم يضعه أحد ولكن كان الحجر راسخاً فتعقل به إنسان ووقع في البئر ومات فالضمان على الحافر لأنه متعد في التسبب وكان بمنزلة الماشي إذا وقع في البئر لم يعلم بالبئر فالضمان على الحافر، وإن كان الماشي دافعاً نفسه في البئر وأنه مباشر والحافر متسبب. وفي الظهيرية : وإن كان الحجر لم يضعه أحد لكنه حميل السيل جاء به فالضمان على الحافر ومن هذا الجنس ما ذكر في المنتقى : رجل حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء إنسان وزلق بما صبه رجل آخر على الطريق ووقع في البئر ومات فالضمان على الذي صب الماء، فإن كان الماء ماء السماء
And if the digging was in the courtyard of his house, and a person fell into it and died, is he liable? If the courtyard belongs to another, he is liable. But if he dug in his own property, or he had the right to dig by ancient right, then similarly the answer is he is not liable. If it is not his property but it is common to Muslims, or it is shared as in a cul-de-sac, then he is liable. Al-Muntaqa said: The courtyard of a man is what is in his house that he needs, even if it is along the width of his alley or wider. As for if he ordered a man to dig a well for him at the base of his neighbor's wall and courtyard, all that is the courtyard of the orderer and the courtyard of his neighbor which is his courtyard, thus it is their courtyard. And if the alley is a dead end, and he ordered digging in a place where he has no benefit and the house does not need it, this is not his courtyard. And if a person throws himself into the well, there is no liability on the digger. Sharh al-Tahawi: Whoever digs a well on the main road and an animal or person falls into it and perishes, the liability is on the digger. And if a person came and pushed him and threw him into the well and he perished, the liability is on the pusher, not the digger. In al-Khaniyya: A man dug a well in his property, then a person fell and killed that person or animal, the fallen one is liable for the diyah or value of whoever was in it. If the well was in the road, the liability is on the digger of the well. So if he dug in his own property, the fallen one is not liable to the digger, and the damage is attributed to the fallen one. And if a man dug a well in the road of Muslims, then another dug another portion in its bottom, then a person fell and died, then by qiyas the first should be liable, and Muhammad took that. The scholars differed on the answer of istihsan: some said the answer is that liability is on the first and the second; some said the answer is that liability is on the second specifically, but our Companions took the qiyas. It is like a man who dug a well on the main road, then a man came and placed a weapon in the well, then a man came and fell on the weapon and died from that: the liability is on the digger. And someone was asked about a man who dug in the desert of a village belonging to the villagers, where their animals sleep, a pit in which he puts wheat and barley without permission of the others, then a man came and lit a fire in the pit, covering it, also without permission, then a donkey fell into it and burned: who is liable? He said: the digger. He said: This is qiyas from what is transmitted from our companions in the book of diyat: a man dug a well on the main road, and a man threw a stone into it after a man fell into the well, and the stone hit him and he died: the diyah is on the digger. Similarly, if a man placed a stone on the ground near the well, and a person stumbled on it and fell into the well and perished, the diyah is on the one who placed the stone, as if he threw him into the well and he died. And if that were so, the liability would be on the pusher, and here too. This is when someone placed the stone. But if no one placed it, but the stone was firm, and a person stumbled on it and fell into the well and died, the liability is on the digger, because he transgressed in causing, and it is like a pedestrian who falls into the well not knowing about it: the liability is on the digger. Even if the pedestrian threw himself into the well, he is a direct agent and the digger is a cause. In al-Zahiriya: If no one placed the stone, but it was carried by a torrent, the liability is on the digger. From this type is what is mentioned in al-Muntaqa: a man dug a well on the main road, then a man came and slipped on water spilled by another man on the road, and fell into the well and died: the liability is on the one who spilled the water. If the water was rainwater...
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage consolidates several rulings on liability for wells, focusing on where the digging occurs and the involvement of third parties. The key principle: a digger is not liable for wells dug on his own private property, because the victim is a trespasser. However, if the well is on a public road or shared property (like a dead-end alley), the digger is liable. The text also defines the courtyard (fina') of a house as the area needed for access, even if part of the alley. If an orderer hires someone to dig on a neighbor's property, it is considered the fina' of both. Additionally, the passage covers cases where a third party pushes someone into the well, or places a stone in the well, or spills water causing a slip. In these cases, liability shifts to the immediate cause (the pusher, the stone placer, the water spiller). However, if the stone is naturally occurring or carried by a torrent, the digger remains liable. The passage also revisits the two-diggers case, reiterating the qiyas view of Muhammad and istihsan views. The overall thrust is to assign liability based on causation and ownership of the land.

Key Terms
الفناء (al-fina'): The courtyard or necessary open space around a house, including part of the alley if needed for access.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to pay compensation.
قارعة الطريق (qari'at al-tariq): Main road or public thoroughfare.
سكة غير نافذة (sikka ghayr nafidha): Cul-de-sac; a dead-end alley shared by residents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on liability when a well is dug in a cul-de-sac (dead-end alley)?
A) No liability because it is private property.
B) Liability because it is shared property.
C) Liability only if the well is deep.
D) No liability if the residents consented.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for a well depends on who owns the land: no liability on private land, full liability on public or shared land.

Third-Party Interference: When Liability Shifts from Digger to Another
Rulings on liability when a third party intervenes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when liability for a well casualty shifts from the digger to a third party, and apply the rule that the direct cause is liable over the indirect cause.

Core Idea
When a third party directly causes the fall, liability transfers, unless the obstruction is natural, then the digger remains liable.

Beyond the basic location-based liability, Hanafi fiqh deals with complex cases where a third party's action intervenes. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq details scenarios involving someone pushing the victim, placing a stone in the well, or spilling water that causes a slip. The general rule: the immediate cause (mubashir) is liable, not the digger (mutasabbib), except when the intermediate cause is natural.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
When a direct cause (mubashir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib) combine, the direct cause is liable. The digger is only liable if no direct agent intervenes.
This is based on the case of the pusher: 'الضمان على الدافع دون الحافر'
2
Examine the pusher case
If a person pushes another into a well on a public road, the pusher is liable, not the digger.
Example from Sharh al-Tahawi.
3
Examine the stone placement case
If someone places a stone in the well after a victim falls, or places a stone near the well causing a stumble, that person is liable. But if the stone is naturally present (or carried by torrent), the digger remains liable.
The text distinguishes between man-made and natural obstructions.
4
Examine the water-spilling case
If someone spills water on the road, causing a person to slip and fall into the well, the water-spiller is liable. If the water is rainwater, the digger is liable.
5
Two-diggers case
When two men dig a well together (one after another), the qiyas view (taken by Muhammad) holds the first digger liable. Some istihsan views split liability. The Hanafi school follows qiyas.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage consolidates several rulings on liability for wells, focusing on where the digging occurs and the involvement of third parties. The key principle: a digger is not liable for wells dug on his own private property, because the victim is a trespasser. However, if the well is on a public road or shared property (like a dead-end alley), the digger is liable. The text also defines the courtyard (fina') of a house as the area needed for access, even if part of the alley. If an orderer hires someone to dig on a neighbor's property, it is considered the fina' of both. Additionally, the passage covers cases where a third party pushes someone into the well, or places a stone in the well, or spills water causing a slip. In these cases, liability shifts to the immediate cause (the pusher, the stone placer, the water spiller). However, if the stone is naturally occurring or carried by a torrent, the digger remains liable. The passage also revisits the two-diggers case, reiterating the qiyas view of Muhammad and istihsan views. The overall thrust is to assign liability based on causation and ownership of the land.

Key Terms
المبشر (al-mubashir): The direct agent who directly causes harm.
المتسبب (al-mutasabbib): The indirect cause who sets up conditions for harm.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Hanafi usul.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method to depart from qiyas for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man spills water on the road, causing a pedestrian to slip and fall into a well dug on the road, who is liable?
A) The digger.
B) The person who spilled the water.
C) Both equally.
D) The pedestrian for not being careful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a third party directly causes a fall into a well, liability shifts to them, but the digger remains liable if the intermediate cause is natural.

Liability for Digging a Pit on a Public Road in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on liability for digging a pit on a public road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for digging a pit on a public road and apply the distinction between direct and indirect causation to determine who is liable.

Core Idea
Digging on public land makes you liable; digging on your own land does not—unless you cause harm indirectly.

This lesson explores the detailed Hanafi rulings on liability when a person digs a pit or well that causes harm. We begin with the foundational principle from Sharh al-Tahawi, then examine exceptions and complex scenarios involving multiple actors and natural causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the location
Determine whether the pit is on a public road (qāriʿat al-ṭarīq), private property, or shared space (fināʾ). This determines the starting point for liability.
2
Apply the general rule
If on a public road, the digger is liable for any person or animal that falls in. If on his own property, the digger is not liable unless the victim is forced by another.
3
Examine the fināʾ definition
Understand what counts as the courtyard of a house and how shared courtyards affect liability, especially in non-through lanes.
4
Analyze successive digging
When multiple diggers are involved (first digs main hole, second digs lower layer), apply qiyas (the later digger is liable) versus istihsan (possible joint liability).
5
Determine causation chains
Distinguish between direct and indirect causes when stones, water, or other objects are involved. The liability shifts to the direct cause if it intervenes.

لم يكن ملكاً له ولكن كان الجماعة المسلمين أو كان شركاً بأن كان في سكة غير نافذة فإنه يضمن. قال في المنتقى : فناء دار الرجل ما كان في داره يحتاج إليه وإن كان في عرض سكته أو أعرض منها، فأما إذا أمر رجلاً أن يحفر له بئراً في أصل حائط جاره وفنائه فهذا كله فناء الآمر وفناء جاره الذي هو فناء له فهو فناؤهما، وإن كانت السكة غير نافذة فأمر بالحفر في موضع ليس له فيه منفعة ولا تحتاج إليه الدار وهذا ليس بفنائه، وإذا أوقع إنسان نفسه في البئر فلا ضمان على الحافر. شرح الطحاوي: ومن حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فوقع فيها دابة أو إنسان فتلف فالضمان على الحافر، ولو جاء إنسان فدفعه وألقاه في البئر وهلك فالضمان على الدافع دون الحافر. وفي الخانية : رجل حفر بئراً في ملكه ثم سقط إنسان فقتل الساقط ذلك الإنسان أو الدابة كان الساقط ضامناً دية أو قيمة من كان فيها، وإن كان البئر في الطريق كان الضمان على حافر البئر، فإذا حفر في ملك نفسه فسقوطه لا يكون ضامناً إلى الحافر وكان تلف السقوط عليه مضافاً إلى الساقط . وإذا حفر الرجل بئراً في طريق المسلمين ثم آخر حفر طائقة أخرى في أسفلها ثم وقع إنسان ومات فإنه ينبغي في القياس أن يضمن وبه أخذ محمد، واختلف المشايخ في جواب الاستحسان فمنهم من قال جواب الاستحسان أن يكون الضمان على الأول والثاني، ومنهم من قال جواب الاستحسان أن يكون الضمان على الثاني خاصة إلا أن أصحابنا أخذوا بالقياس وكان كمن حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء إنسان ووضع في البئر سلاحاً ثم جاء إنسان ووقع على السلاح ومات من ذلك فإن الضمان على الحافر وسئل بعضهم عمن حفر في صحراء قرية التي لأهل القرية وهي مبيت دوابهم حفيرة يضع فيها الحنطة والشعير بغير إذن الباقين فجاء رجل وأوقد في الحفيرة ناراً كستها وذلك أيضاً بغير إذن الباقين فوقع فيها حمار فاحترق بالنار فالضمان على من يجب ؟ فقال : على الحافر. قال : وهذا قياس ما نقل عن أصحابنا في كتاب الديات حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق وألقى رجل فيها حجراً بعدما وقع في البئر رجل فأصابه الحجر الذي في البئر فمات أن الدية على الحافر، ومثله لو وضع رجل حجراً على الأرض بقرب البئر فتعقل فيها إنسان ووقع فهلك فالدية على من وضع الحجر كأنه ألقاه في البئر فمات، ولو كان كذلك كان الضمان على الدافع وكذلك ههنا هذا إذا وضع الحجر واضع، فأما إذا لم يضعه أحد ولكن كان الحجر راسخاً فتعقل به إنسان ووقع في البئر ومات فالضمان على الحافر لأنه متعد في التسبب وكان بمنزلة الماشي إذا وقع في البئر لم يعلم بالبئر فالضمان على الحافر، وإن كان الماشي دافعاً نفسه في البئر وأنه مباشر والحافر متسبب. وفي الظهيرية : وإن كان الحجر لم يضعه أحد لكنه حميل السيل جاء به فالضمان على الحافر ومن هذا الجنس ما ذكر في المنتقى : رجل حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء إنسان وزلق بما صبه رجل آخر على الطريق ووقع في البئر ومات فالضمان على الذي صب الماء، فإن كان الماء ماء السماء فعلى صاحب البئر.
If it was not his property but was the community of Muslims or was shared, such as in a non-through road, then he is liable. Al-Muntaqa said: The courtyard of a man is what is in his house that he needs, even if it is in the width of his lane or beyond. As for when he orders a man to dig a well for him at the base of his neighbor's wall and courtyard, all of that is the courtyard of the one commanding and the courtyard of his neighbor which is his courtyard, so it is their courtyard. If the lane is non-through and he ordered digging in a place where he has no benefit and the house does not need it, this is not his courtyard. If a person throws himself into the well, there is no liability on the digger. Sharh al-Tahawi: Whoever digs a well on the main road and an animal or person falls into it and dies, the liability is on the digger. If a person comes and pushes him and throws him into the well and he perishes, the liability is on the pusher, not the digger. In al-Khaniyya: A man dug a well on his property, then a person fell and killed that person or animal, the fallen person is liable for the blood money or value of whoever was in it. If the well was on the road, the liability is on the digger of the well. If he dug on his own property, his falling does not make the digger liable, and the loss due to falling is attributed to the fallen one. If a man dug a well on the Muslims' road, then another dug another layer at its bottom, then a person fell and died, according to qiyas he is liable, and Muhammad adopted this. The later scholars differed in the answer of istihsan: some said it should be liability on both the first and second, others said liability only on the second. However, our scholars adopted qiyas, and it is like one who dug a well on the main road, then a person came and placed a weapon in the well, then another came and fell on the weapon and died of that; the liability is on the digger. Some were asked about one who dug in a village's desert that belongs to the villagers and is the resting place of their animals, a pit to put wheat and barley without permission of the rest, then a man came and lit a fire in the pit and covered it, also without permission, then a donkey fell and burned; on whom is the liability? He said: on the digger. He said: This is analogous to what our scholars transmitted in the Book of Diyat: one dug a well on the main road, a man threw a stone into the well after a man had fallen into the well, and the stone hit the one in the well and he died; the blood money is on the digger. Similarly, if a man placed a stone on the ground near the well, and a person tripped on it, fell into the well, and died, the blood money is on the one who placed the stone, as if he threw him into the well and he died. If it was like that, the liability is on the pusher. Here, this is when the stone was placed by someone. However, if no one placed it but the stone was fixed, and a person tripped on it and fell into the well and died, the liability is on the digger because he transgressed in causation and is like the pedestrian who falls into the well not knowing about the well; the liability is on the digger. Even if the pedestrian pushed himself into the well, he is a direct cause and the digger is an indirect cause. In al-Zuhayriyya: If the stone was not placed by anyone but was brought by the flood current, the liability is on the digger. Of this type is what is mentioned in al-Muntaqa: A man dug a well on the main road, then a man came and slipped due to water that another man had poured on the road and fell into the well and died; the liability is on the one who poured the water. If the water was rainwater, then on the owner of the well.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, presents rulings on liability for harm caused by digging wells or pits. It begins by establishing that if the pit is on shared or public property (like a non-through lane), the digger is liable. The 'Fina' (courtyard) of a house is defined as the area within the house needed by the owner. If a person orders digging on another's property with permission, the courtyard may be shared. If the digging is in a place where the owner has no benefit, it is not considered his courtyard, and if someone throws himself in, no liability. The core ruling from Sharh al-Tahawi states: digging a well on the main road makes the digger liable for any person or animal that falls in and dies. However, if someone is pushed by another, the pusher bears liability. If the well is on one's own property, the digger is not liable; rather, the person who falls and causes harm is liable. The passage then discusses a scenario where two persons dig successively—the first digs the main hole, the second digs a lower layer. According to qiyas (analogy), the second is liable, and Imam Muhammad adopted this. Later scholars differed on istihsan (juristic preference): some say both are liable, others only the second. The passage then gives several examples to illustrate causation: if a stone is placed by someone, that person is liable; if the stone is fixed naturally, the digger remains liable as an indirect cause. Similarly, if water is poured on the road, the pourer is liable; if it is rainwater, the well owner is liable. Throughout, the principle is that liability follows the direct or indirect cause depending on the circumstances.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for harm caused, whether through direct or indirect causation.
قارعة الطريق (qāriʿat al-ṭarīq): The main public road or highway where people and animals frequently pass.
فناء (fināʾ): The courtyard or immediate surrounding area of a house that is considered part of the property for usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man digs a well on a public road and a person is pushed by another into the well and dies, who is liable for the blood money?
A) The digger
B) The pusher
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school imposes liability on the digger of a pit on public roads for any harm caused, but this liability is negated if the victim is directly caused by another or if the pit is on private property.

Liability for Hiring Digging in Public Roads: Between Qiyas and Istihsan
Liability rules for hiring digging in roads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a hirer orders digging in a road, apply the distinction between qiyas and istihsan in the fina' scenario, and identify the factors that shift liability from the hirer to the worker.

Core Idea
Liability follows the transgressor, not the doer — istihsan overrides qiyas when the hirer has apparent authority.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Book of Blood Money on liability when a person hires workers to dig in a road. The Hanafi school balances analogical reasoning (qiyas) with juristic preference (istihsan) to determine whether the hirer or the worker is liable for resulting damages. Through a series of cases, you will learn how knowledge, the nature of the road, and the concept of fina' (courtyard) affect liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Determine the scenario: a hirer (musta'jir) orders a hired worker (ajir) to dig in a road or fina'. Note each party's role and knowledge.
The initial ruling places liability on the hirer because he is the transgressor (muta'addi).
2
Apply the default rule: hirer's liability
By default, the hirer is liable because he gives the order in a place where he has no right to dig. The worker is merely carrying out orders and is not transgressing.
This is based on causation (tasabbub) and preponderance of transgression.
3
Identify the exception: when the worker knows
If the worker knows that the order is invalid (e.g., the road is public), the liability shifts to the worker because there is no deception (ghurur) from the hirer.
Knowledge eliminates the hirer's deception, so the act is attributed to the worker.
4
Understand the istihsan for fina'
When the hirer orders digging in his fina' (courtyard that is effectively his), qiyas would make the worker liable if he knew the fina' was not owned. But by istihsan, the hirer is liable because the fina' is treated like owned property due to the hirer's free disposition (yad mutlaqa).
Istihsan considers custom ('urf) and the hirer's apparent authority to override strict analogy.
